with their bodies appearing radiant, splendid and pleasing. However,
too much focus on the details can be an obstacle. What is most important
is to occupy your mind with extraordinary thoughts and feel a strong
connection with the lineage, thinking that all of these holy beings are
actually present. You can bring to mind each lineage master as you recite
the prayer, and it can help to recall details of their stories while you do
this. Practising in this way creates a link between you and the precious
lineage, and the lineage connects you with the sacred truth of your own
Buddha-nature.
I pay homage and pray to my root lama.
I pray to the root and lineage lamas.
I pray to the wish-fulfilling lineage.
Paying homage and praying to the root and lineage lamas is a way of
showing our deepest honour and respect, reminding ourselves of how
precious this spiritual relationship is. The Tibetan word for root lama
is 'Dinchen Lama', and this refers to the Dharma teacher or teachers
you have the most gratitude towards, who have personally shown you
the path to liberation. Of all the teachers you have encountered, your
root lama is whoever you regard as the most important, whoever you
have received the greatest number of teachings from, or whoever has
benefited you the most in an enlightened way. This can be one lama or
many lamas.
1
The other lineage masters may not have taught you directly, yet they
are an integral part of the transmission lineage. Without this lineage
of transmission enlightenment cannot be attained, and therefore the
lineage is like a wish-fulfilling gem, which grants any wish that a person
desires. You should therefore feel deep humility and gratitude towards
these masters, even if you haven't met them, in order to make a spiritual
connection with this holy lineage.
Please bless me so the lineage transmission will enter within me.
May all these blessings enter my heart!
Please bless me so the darkness in my heart be cleared away!
As explained previously, you receive blessings when your own good
qualities increase, or when you become closer to the reality of your
Buddha-nature. The transmission lineage is like a staircase that helps
you uncover this Buddha-nature, leading to deep transformation as the
185



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
L
E
:--
--
blessings enter your heart. This is much more than a temporary ‘feel-
good' practice, as it can clear away the darkness of ignorance and other
defilements that the jewel of Buddha-nature is currently wrapped up in.
I pray to the lama.
I pray to the lord of Dharma.
May all the spiritual fathers and their heart-sons bless me!
As mentioned before, the lama is someone “above oneself' who is
superior in spiritual qualities and therefore worthy of prayer and homage.
Lord of Dharma means they are like a king of spirituality. Heart-sons
refer to those in the lineage who are close disciples of great lamas, who
are their spiritual fathers. They are like a prince who will ascend to their
master's throne to continue their work. For example, the great Tibetan
saint Milarepa was the heart-son of Marpa, and the great Shogyalwa was
one of Dolpopa's fourteen heart-sons.
I pray to the Tathagataghaba, the essence
of the primordial ground.
I pray to the profound Kalachakra vajra path.
I pray to the unveiled dharmakaya truth body,
the fruit of the exhaustion of samsara.
The Tathagataghaba refers to the fully awakened enlightened mind
of Buddhahood, whose essence resides in all beings as the primordial
ground of enlightenment, yet is currently obscured by temporary
defilements. Buddha Maitreya likens this primordial ground to a treasure
underground, honey amidst bees, a grain in its husk or a precious image
under a layer of clay. The profound Kalachakra vajra path refers to
the teachings and practices you must follow to awaken this true nature
according to the Kalachakra Tantra, and this includes all the preliminary
practices described in the Divine Ladder as well as the main practice of
the Six Vajra Yogas.
The unveiled dharmakaya truth body is the final result of following the
path, at which point all afflictions are completely purified and realisation
of Buddhahood is attained. Although the ground and the result are
inseparable, on a relative level we need to practice the path in order to
unwrap the many layers of defilements which prevent us from seeing
this truth.
The dharmakaya is one of three bodies or dimensions of enlightenment
186



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
-
-
.
(kaya in Tibetan). It refers to the permanent, unchanging, empty aspect
of the enlightened mind, like the experience of an actor which only can
be perceived from his point of view and not from the point of view
of others. The other dimensions, which are mentioned shortly, are the
samboghakaya enjoyment body and nirmanakaya emanation body.
I pray to the sublime primordial Buddha.
I pray to the dharmakaya truth body Vajradhara.
Primordial Buddha and Vajradhara are different names to describe the
dharmakaya truth body of enlightenment. Each of these names points to
a different aspect of this sacred truth which is completely beyond any
attempt to conceptualise it. This is similar to the many different names
used to describe the different roles you play in different circumstances -
for example as a doctor, a husband or a first-born child.
Primordial Buddha means beginningless, timeless and never stained by
the relative truth or the afflictions of samsara, just as space pervades all
other elements yet is not in the slightest way affected by them. Vajradhara
is similar to the primordial Buddha, only with more of a concept of
physical form. Although Vajradhara refers to a specific enlightened being
with a specific form, in reality he is completely beyond form, space and
time, and therefore he symbolises the dharmakaya truth body.
I pray to the sambhogakaya enjoyment body Kalachakra.
In the root text longku means samboghakaya form, which is the subtlest
and purest display of the dharmakaya, also known as the enjoyment
body. To continue with the example of the actor, the samboghakaya is
like the actor naked, the purest and rawest display of his performance
which is perceived only by those with the purest vision. In the same
way the Buddha's enjoyment body can only be perceived by beings with
extraordinarily pure perception, known as 10th level bodhisattvas.
Dukyi Korlo is Tibetan for Kälachakra, which is literally translated as
'Wheel of Time’. This is a kind of 'timeless time' which is beyond our
ordinary concept of time – the only way to explain this is to use the
metaphor of a wheel, which has no beginning and no end. The Kalachakra
Tantra describes in great detail the laws that govern the cycles of the
outer universe and how these are related to the inner universe of the
187



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
physical body, and it is also linked to astrology. Through the power of
this state which is beyond time, we can come to know details of events
that occur in ordinary time with great precision 15.
When the Buddha first taught the Kalachakra Tantra, he manifested in
the form of Kalachakra along with a mandala of 722 deities at Amaravati
in Southern India, to an audience which included multitudes of humans
and non-humans. The main recipient of these teachings was King
Suchandra, the great Dharma King who transmitted these teachings to
the mythical human realm known as Shambala. Through the power of
these teachings he was able to create a form of spirituality which would
unite people with different religious backgrounds and bring peace to his
kingdom.
Only beings with extremely high spiritual attainment, such as King
Suchandra, could perceive and experience the enlightened form of
Kalachakra directly. We can therefore say that Buddha Shakyamuni
appeared in sambhogakaya form as Kalachakra when he taught the
Kalachakra Tantra.
I pray to the nirmanakaya emanation body, Buddha Shakyamuni.
On a simple level, the nirmanakaya emanation body is the human being
we know as Prince Siddhartha, who showed how an ordinary human
being could become a completely awakened Buddha, in this case Buddha
Shakyamuni. Buddha means awakened one and Shakya refers to the
name of his clan. On a deeper level, the nirmanakaya emanation body is
the reflection of the sambhogakaya, on this occasion appearing in human
form and displaying a life with birth, ageing and death according to the
karmic vision of human beings. Therefore, unlike the sambhogakaya
enjoyment body, even ordinary beings can perceive the nirmakakaya
form of the Buddha.
15 The Kalachakra Tantra is divided into three sections: Outer Kalachakra (to
do with the cycles of the external universe, astrology and so forth), Inner Kalachakra
(to do with the cycles and make-up of the subtle body) and Other Kalachakra (the
path to enlightenment according to the Kalachakra system). These teachings are
described in great detail in: Khedrup Norsang Gyatso. Ornament of Stainless Light: An
Exposition of the Kalachakra Tantra. Boston: Wisdom 2004.
188



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
To continue with our example of the actor, the nirmanakaya is like the
appearance of the actor to the main audience as he dresses in different
costumes – as a dancer, a king or a prince - in order to play different
roles. In the same way countless emanation bodies manifest from the
Buddha's compassion, which can be perceived according to the merit or
karmic vision of different beings.
I pray to the thirty-five Dharma Kings of Shambala.
Shambala is a mythical human realm which is related to a specific place
on earth, said to be in the vicinity of the Himalayas. Unlike Sukhavati,
the heavenly realm of the Buddha Amitabha, it is related to our universe.
However, for ordinary people it is not a physical experience – only
certain practitioners can perceive it with special conditions including a
certain window of time, great merit and high spiritual attainment. After
King Suchandra, who received the Kalachakra Tantra directly from the
Buddha, there is a line of 34 succeeding Shambala kings, known as
Dharma Kings, who taught millions the Kalachakra practice and will
continue to teach until the end of this dysnasty16.
This lineage is said to continue in the present day, with the 14th Kulika
king, Rigden Magakpa, having ascended to the throne in 1927. The
Kalachakra Tantra also gives a prophecy that during the reign of the
25th Kulika, Rigden Raudra Chakri, a period of violence and unrest
will take over the world due to the actions of extremists, motivated by
extreme greed and materialism. Around 2424 C.E., a magical army will
manifest through the enlightened power of Raudra Chakri, defeating the
extremists and opening the door to a golden age of 1,800 years, during
which the Kalachakra Tantra will flourish in the human world.
I pray to the Drupchen Dushapa Chenpo.
Drupchen Dushapa Chenpo, also known as Kalachakrapada the Elder,
was the first holder of our Kalachakra lineage in the human world. Son
of a Brahmin yogi, Dushapa ordained and studied at Nalanda University
in India. The Kalachakra teachings started to spread to India during
16 More details of this Shambala Kalachakra lineage can be found in chapter
one of Unveiling Your Sacerd Truth and numerous other sources. See, for example:
Khedrup Norsang Gyatso. Ornament of Stainless Light: An Exposition of the
Kalachakra Tantra.
189



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
the reign of the 11th Shambala King Dakpa-Yashas, and at this time
Drupchen Dushapa met an emanation of King Suchandra and traveled
to Shambala. On arriving there, he was greeted by the Dharma King and
was given the Kalachakra empowerment, along with commentaries of
the Kalachakra Tantra and practice instructions.
Dushapa memorised and brought these precious teachings back to India in
the 11th century, thus becoming the 12th holder of the Kalachakra lineage.
Subsequently he attained complete enlightenment through the power
of the Kalachakra practice. Literally Drupchen means 'Mahasiddha',
someone with a high level of spiritual attainment, and Chenpo means
'great'.
I pray to the Drupchen Dushapa Nyipa the second.
Dushapa’s main disciple was a lay person, born of royal caste, known
as Drupchen Dushapa Nyipa. Nyipa means the second'. He was also
known as Sri Badra (skt.) or Kalachakrapada the second.
Dushapa Nyipa experienced visions of many deities and enlightened
realms in his spiritual practice. He once challenged and defeated 500
scholars in debate at Nalanda University, the great centre for Buddhist
learning in ancient India, who had not previously heard of Kalachakra
Tantra. He and his twelve disciples all attained the rainbow body, a sign
that they all accomplished complete enlightenment.
I pray to Gyaltse Nalendrapa.
Gyaltse Nalendrapa, also called Bodhibhadra, was an abbot of Nalanda
University and a disciple of Dushapa Nyipa. An incredibly accomplished
practitioner, he is said to have attained the four sublime powers through
tantric practice – making peace, expanding possibilities, controlling
situations and subduing negativities.
I pray to Panchen Dawa Gonpo.
Panchen Dawa Gonpo, also known as Panchen Somanatha, was originally
of Islamic background. A brilliant scholar at Nalanda university, it was
said that he could memorise sixteen verses in one cycle of breathing. He
190



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
mastered all the sutras and tantras, yet he held the Kalachakra Tantra as
his most precious treasure and accomplished the fourth stage of the Six
Vajra Yogas. It was he who first transmitted the Kalachakra teachings
to Tibet where he taught Droton Lotasawa and Lama Lhaje Gompa.
Panchen literally means 'highly accomplished scholar'.
I pray to the great translator Droton Lotsawa.
Born in western Tibet, Droton Lotsawa had many great pandita teachers;
however, he regarded Panchen Dawagonpo as his main guru. He made
an incredible contribution to the lineage in Tibet by translating all the
teachings of Panchen Dawa Gonpo, both written and oral, and was thus
widely regarded as a great translator. He spent the later part of his life
close to Dawa Gonpo until the time of his death.
I pray to Lama Lhaje Gompa.
Lama Lhaje Gompa was born in western Tibet and was the most
successful in spreading the Kalachakra lineage widely. He was a highly
accomplished tantric practitioner in the Nyingma tradition and practised
as a nagpa, a lay practitioner. He received many teachings, especially
from Panchen Dawa Gonpo, and was known for his ability to pacify two
demons who were said to possess powers of black magic.
I pray to Lama Droton Namseg.
Lama Droton Namseg was a nagpa wearing white robes, who received
the Kalachakra transmission from Lama Lhaje Gompa. A great scholar,
his studies included Asanga's Five Collections and Nagarjuna's Six
Madhyamika Treatises, as well as other subjects such as literature and
medicine. His heart practice, however, was the Kalachakra Tantra. He
is said to have had a direct connection with numerous yidam deities,
and the enlightened dakinis would assist him whenever he needed their
help.
I pray to the Lama Dupchen Yumo.
I pray to Seachok Dharmeshvara.
I pray to Khipa Namkha Öser.
Lama Dupchen Yumo was born in a region of Tibet near the Himalayas.
A fully ordained monk, he was highly regarded as a pure holder of the
191



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
Vinaya, the monastic code of discipline, and he also studied a variety of
tantric teachings. He received the Kalachakra transmission from Panchen
Dawa Gonpo and practised extensively for six years under the guidance
of Lama Droton Namseg. He accomplished remarkable powers such as
the ability to manifest in different forms and was renowned for his great
scholastic knowledge of the Kalachakra Tantra; thus he became well-
known throughout Tibet and neighbouring regions as well.
Seachok Dharmeshvara was Drupchen Yumo's son. An exceptional
scholar, by the age of sixteen he wrote a commentary on an advanced
Buddhist text (known as Wang Dorten) and by twenty he comprehended
everything his father knew. It was said that he was an emanation of
Manjushri, as he mastered every detail of the sutras and tantras and
many renowned scholars were defeated by his logic. Dharmeshvara
· received teachings from many lamas and was particularly drawn to
the Guhyasamaja and Kalachakra Tantras. Literally Seachock means
‘supreme son’.
Khipa Namkha Öser was born in Kangsar and was Seachok
Dharmeshvara's spiritual heart-son. A nagpa tantric yogi and scholar,
he especially focused on Asanga's Five Collections as well as the
Guhyasamaja and Kalachakra Tantras, and he composed many texts. It
was said that he had a direct connection to the female deities Vajravarahi
and Sarasvati. Literally Kipa means 'extraordinary scholar'.
I pray to Machig Tulku Jobum.
I pray to Lama Drubtop Sechen.
I pray to the Chöje Jamyang Sarma.
Machig Tulku Jobum was Dharmeshvara's daughter, and was considered
the reincarnation of Indrabhuti's sister!7. She knew the great commentary
of Kalachakra Tantra by heart, and after learning the Kalachakra practice
from her father she attained the ten auspicious signs in one day. After
seven days she could direct the inner wind into her central channel and
she thus became a great yogini, a highly realised female practitioner.
LUI
Lama Drubtop Sechen was born with a speech and hearing impairment,
and no-one believed his life would amount to anything. However, after
17 Indrabhuti was the king of Orissa and was famed as a great tantric
mahasiddha.
192



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
receiving the instructions for the Six Vajra Yogas from Machig Tulku.
Jobum and practising under the guidance of Namkha Öser, he attained
swift realisation, including the ability to recall his previous lives and
know his future lives. Later in his life he established Tsang Orlang
Semoche monastery.
Chöje Jamyang Sarma was born into a Nyingma family, yet after he
ordained he studied at many different monasteries. He contracted
leprosy, however, and then undertook an extensive Vajrapani retreat to
overcome his illness. While on retreat he saw a vision of Manjushri,
who told him to seek instruction from Lama Drubtop Sechen, yet on
his journey to meet Lama Sechen he had to overcome many demons
and obstructive forces. While receiving empowerment he perceived his
lama in the form of Kalachakra, and thereafter he practised the Six Vajra
Yogas and attained great realisation. Chöje literally means 'Dharma
lord' or 'sovereign of the Dharma'.
I pray to Kunkyen Chöku Öser.
I pray to Kunphang Thukje Tsundu.
I pray to Changsem Gyalwa Yeshe.
Kunkyen Chöku Öser was the son of Serlingpa Jonyuot. It was predicted
that he would have the ability to dwell in the dharmakaya state and he was
thus given the name Chöku Öser. A great scholar of the sutras and tantras,
he later developed incredible realisations after receiving the Kalachakra
empowerment and instructions from Jamyang Sarma. For instance, he
was able to directly perceive the wrathful form of Kalachakra, and he
once was seen circumambulating a stupa while meditating in a sealed
room. Kunkyen literally means omniscient, or knowing everything.
Kunphang Thukje Tsundu was born in 1243 and is considered an
emanation of one of the Shambhala. Rigden Kings. He was the first lama
of the ‘Jonang' tradition, which was named after the area ‘Jomonang'
where the first Jonang monastery was established. Initially receiving
instructions according to the Kalachakra Dro lineage from Chöku Öser,
Thukje Tsundu then received teachings from all 17 Kalachakra lineages
in Tibet and combined them into one complete set of teachings and
practices. This is what is known today as the Jonang Kalachakra lineage.
In addition, he composed the first detailed written instructions on the
Six Vajra Yogas and was also known for his vast knowledge and skill in
193



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
debate. His realisation of the Six Yoga practice was also remarkable, and
there are stories of houses shaking and immense heat or cold radiating
from his body as people approached him. Kunphang is a title which
means complete renunciation of all wordly concerns.
Changsem Gyalwa Yeshe ordained and practised Dharma for many years,
yet his attainments were limited. His faith suddenly arose upon meeting
Kunphan Thukje Tsundu, and he progressed swiftly after receiving
empowerment and instructions for the Six Vajra Yogas. Eventually his
realisations equalled that of his teacher, and he then began to spread the
Dharma widely. He was appointed as the abott of Dechen Monastery and
later on he became the head of Jonang monastery. Changsem Gyalwa
means 'Great Bodhisattva'.
I pray to Khetsun Yonten Gyatso.
I pray to Kunkyen Dolpopa, emanation of the Buddhas
of the three times.
I pray to the Chogyal Choglė Namgyal.
I pray to Tsungmed Nyabon Kunga.
Khetsun Yonten Gyatso was born into a family who followed the
Nyingma tradition and studied under many tantric masters from a variety
of monasteries. After receiving the Kalachakra instructions from Thukje
Tsundu he completed all the night yoga practices in one night. While
practising the day yogas, his body shot off into the distance like an arrow
and the surrounding countryside became invisible as a result of his
intense practice. He then developed extraordinary clairvoyant powers and
supreme knowledge of the Buddha's teachings, while his body was said
to emit a beautiful fragrance as a result of his excellent moral conduct.
Yonten Gyatso was a close Dharma friend of Gyalwa Yeshe and later
became his Dharma heir, being appointed abbot of Jonang monastery.
Literally Khetsun means “scholar with excellent moral conduct'.
Kunkyen Dolpopa was considered an emanation of the Buddhas of the
three times as his realisation and mastery of the Buddha's teachings was
so profound, and everyone in Utsang province regarded him as their
master. After gaining supreme realisation in Kalachakra retreat practice,
he developed the incomparable Shentong view and became the fourth
abbot of Jonang monastery. There he developed a system of Buddhist
study and practice which combined study of the Shentong view with
194



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
variety old on the title wived the Ka
retreat practice focusing on the Six Vajra Yogas. This system has been
the precious jewel of the Jonang tradition, remaining intact to the present
time.
Chogyal Choglé Namgyal was born in Tsakarong, and received many
high teachings from his father and uncle while still quite young. He
amazed everyone by giving large public teachings while still a child, and
studied at a variety of different monasteries. As he was always victorious
in debate, he was given the title Choglé Chogyalwa, meaning "The
Invincible One’. Choglé Namgyal received the Kalachakra empowerment
and instructions from Dolpopa and became one his closest students,
perfectly memorising all the great texts. Eventually he became the fifth
abbot of Jonang monastery, and at one point the great Gelug master
Tsongkhapa received Kalachakra teachings from him. Chogyal means
‘Dharma King' while Chogle means 'victorious in all directions'.
Tsungmed Nyabon Kunga showed great intelligence from a young
age. However, his monastic study and practice suffered a setback as
he was paralysed in his early twenties, yet he was miraculously cured
when Choglé Namgyal (towards whom he felt great devotion) spat
on him. Thereafter Dolpopa became his main master and he practiced
diligently under the guidance of Choglé Namgyal. Nyabon Kunga wrote
prolifically, with many of his writings still treasured today, and many
realised practitioners from other traditions received teachings from him,
including Sakya Rendawa and Lama Tsongkhapa. Later in his life he
founded Tsechan Monastery. Tsungmed literally means “incomparable'.
I pray to Drupchen Kunga Lodrö.
I pray to Jamyang Konchog Zangpo.
I pray to Drenchog Namkha Tsenchan.
Drupchen Kunga Lodrö was born into the royal Shawa family and was
believed to be the reincarnation of Buthon Rinpoche. He studied the
Buddha's teachings extensively under Nyabon Kunga, including the
Kalachakra Tantra, and he also studied with many other masters. Having
completely renounced attachment to worldly possessions and status, he
became a homeless wandering yogi and dedicated his life to pursuing
greater realisations. He was widely regarded as a great yogi, yet this did
not mean he was completely removed from the world - in fact he once
acted as a peacemaker between two regions at war with one another.
195



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
Jamyang Konchog Zangpo was born in Latod Chung and was believed
to be the reincarnation of the great Sakyapa Drakpa Gyaltsen. He trained
at Zangden Monastery as well as many other monasteries from a variety
of traditions, especially Sakya. Becoming a great scholar, he received
the Kalachakra transmission from Kunga Lodrö and this became his
heart practice. Later in his life he founded Nyangtod Monastery and he
directed three monasteries in the Jonang tradition, becoming the seventh
abbot of Jonang Monastery. He was also known for his ability to pacify
demons or perform exorcisms.

Drenchog Namkha Tsenchan studied at Jamyang Konchok's monastery
as well as other monasteries in central Tibet, and he quickly mastered
the Shentong view and practice of the Six Vajra Yogas. He achieved
great realisation through the Six Yoga practice and became the abbot
of Tsechen Monastery. He was also responsible for constructing a gold-
plated roof for the great stupa at the main Jonang retreat centre. Literally
Drenchog means “supreme rescuer'.
I pray to the Panchen Namkha Palzang.
I pray to Lochen Ratnabhadra.
I pray to Palden Kunga Dolchog.
The great Panchen Namkha Palzang was originally from the Sakya
tradition. He became an expert in the Kalachakra Tantra, receiving
empowerment and instructions from Drenchog Namkha. He attained
great realisation by practising the Six Vajra Yogas and was the founder
of Drepung Monastery in central Tibet, as well as being the ninth abbot
of Jonang monastery. Panchen literally means great pandita or scholar.
Lochen Ratnabhadra was a great scholar of the Nyingma tantras.
Training at several large monasteries, he became a respected scholar and
also achieved great realisation after receiving the Kalachakra teachings
from Namkha Palzang. It was said that he had a direct connection to the
wrathful deity Mahakala and was able to pacify many demons. Later in
his life Ratnabhadra established several monasteries and retreat centres,
and composed an important commentary on the Six Vajra Yogas. Lochen
literally means “great translator (lotasawa in Tib.)'.
Palden Kunga Dolchog was born in Ngari Gongtung and lived between
1507 and 1566. He mastered many advanced teachings at a young age

and studied with a many great scholars in central Tibet. He had a close

Jangtse Monastery and he also directed Jonang Monastery. He wrote
a text known as the Jonang Tridgya, which summarised all Tibetan

I pray to Kanchen Lungrig Gyatso.
I pray to Kyabdak Drolway Gonpo.
I pray to Ngonjang Rinchen Gyatso.
Kanchen Lungrig Gyatso, also known as Kanchen Gyatso Dejé, trained
at Chulong Jangste Monastery and later became Kunga Dolchog's main
student. His main deity was Vajrayogini, who became his unfailing guide
after their meeting in a dream. He was also a great Kalachakra practitioner
who had direct visions of many deities and yoginis, and swiftly, attained
the ten signs of Kalachakra practice. In addition, he acquired knowledge
of Sanskrit and other Indian languages without ever having studied
them, and it was believed that he received pure transmissions from the
original Indian tantric masters. The ninth Karmapa Wangchuk Dorjé
and Sakyapa Dukshin both gave him the title “Treasury of Dharma'.
Later in his life Lungrig Gyatso built Takten Phuntsok Ling monastery,
and finally he passed into the pure realm of Medicine Buddha. Kanchen
means 'great khenpo', someone who is both an accomplished scholar
and monastic leader.
Kyabdak Kunga Nyingpo (Jetsun Taranatha in Skt. and also known
as Drolway Gonpo) lived between 1575 and 1635, and is considered
one of the most important Jonang lineage masters. Among his many
accomplishments, he remembered one of his previous lives as the Indian
Mahasiddha Drupchen Nakpopa and wrote a history of Dharma in India
based on his recollections. Nowadays this text is considered the most
accurate in its field and is still widely used today. Taranatha was a holder
of all lineages of Vajrayana Buddhism and composed about 40 Buddhist
texts. He founded Takten Phuntsok Ling Monastery and benefited beings
in countless other ways. Kyabdak literally means 'all-pervasive rescuer

Ngonjang Rinchen Gyatso was born in the Tsang region and was ordained
by Taranatha. He progressed swiftly in the Kalachakra practice and as a
sign of his attainment he was suddenly able to absorb large volumes of
knowledge instantly. Becoming Abbot of Takten Damcholing Monastery,
he taught extensively and guided retreat practice at the monastery for
15 years. Later he devoted his life to guiding genuine practitioners at a
retreat centre called Sangngak Riwo Dechen. Literally ngonjang means
‘accomplished due to training in previous lives'.
I pray to Khidrup Lödro Namgyal.
I pray to Drupchen Ngawang Trinley.
I pray to Ngawang Tenzin Namgyal.
Khidrup Lödro Namgyal lived between 1618 and 1683. He was
recognised as a reincarnation of Dolpopa's mother and became a student
of Taranatha when 16 years old. On one occasion he discussed his
realisation of the Shentong view with the great fifth Dalai Lama, who
it was said was very impressed. After many years of Dharma practice
he received full ordination from Rinchen Gyatso, and was guided by
visions of White Tara after receiving this empowerment from him. Later
in his life Lödro Namgyal taught the Kalachakra Tantra at Dzamthang
Monastery. Whenever he performed the wrathful Mahakala ritual,
miraculous signs such as spontaneous flames would appear. Khidrup
literally means 'scholar-yogi', someone very learned and also realised.
Drupchen Ngawang Trinley lived between 1657 and 1713. Dolpopa had
prophesied his coming, predicting that he would have a great impact on
the spread of authentic Dharma. At sixteen years of age he became the
regent of Lödro Namgyal and practised the Six Vajra Yogas under his
guidance. He went into retreat for six years in the cave of Amitabha,
and afterwards he taught and travelled widely, becoming the director of
many monasteries, guiding Kalachakra retreat practice and composing
many texts such as the recitation of the seven Kalachakra preliminaries.
He also received teachings from lamas of all traditions and became
widely known as a great Rimé master. Later in his life Ngawang Trinley
taught and established several monasteries in Mongolia at the request of
the emperor.

ern
Ngawang Tenzin Namgyal, also known as Gawi Chöpel, was born in 1690
and received the Kalachakra Tantra from Ngawang Trinley when he was
ten years old. At 16 he ordained, and later moved to Tsangwa Monastery
(a branch of Dzamthang Monastery) where he began to teach the Six
Vajra Yogas. Many students attained visions and other realisations under
his guidance. He was highly influential in society at that time, much
like Dolpopa, but passed away in 1738 aged only 48 years. Literally
Ngawang means 'great scholar endowed with powerful speech'.
I pray to Ngawang Khetsun Dargyay.
I pray to Kunzang Trinley Namgyal.
I pray to Nuden Lhundrub Gyatso.
Ngawang Khetsun Dargyay was the second Kalachakra lineage holder
at Tsangwa Monastery. He was renowned for his vast knowledge of
Dharma and his display of perfect moral conduct, as well as his deep
inner realisation. He especially had great insight into the practice of the
Six Vajra Yogas, and had several great disciples such as Kunga Chöpel
and Chayur Chöjor.
S
Kunzang Trinley Namgyal was born in eastern Tibet, his coming being
foretold by Taranatha several centuries earlier. From a young age
made a connection with many holy beings, including his root lama
Ngawang Khetsun Dargyay. He received innumerable empowerments
and instructions, and attained extraordinary realisation through diligent
practice of the Six Vajra Yogas. Even the Karmapa, one of the highest
lamas in all of Tibet, travelled a great distance from Utsang province
in order to visit and receive teachings from him. Kunzang means
“possessing all good qualities'.
Nuden Lhundrub Gyatso was the most influentilal disciple of Kunzang
Trinley Namgyal. He was highly accomplished in the practice of inner
heat (tummo)18 and developed invincible wrathful tantric power, through
which he was able to control all the local demons and deities.
18 The practice of inner heat, tummo in Tibetan, involves. focusing on the
navel-centre and holding one's breath here after each inhalation. As an outward sign of
progress, practitioners would feel great warmth over the navel-centre and could survive
with minimal clothing in freezing temperatures

BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
He also established Lower Tsangwa Monastery, performing many great
Dharma activities with the help of Jinpa Gyatso (the second reincarnation
of Ngawang Trinley). The present-day reincarnation of Lhundrub Gyatso
heads a monastery in the Golok region of Tibet. Nuden literally means
‘possessing great energy and healing power'.
I pray to Konchok Jigmé Namgyal.
I pray to Ngawang Chöpel Gyatso.
I pray to Ngawang Chökyi Pakpa.
Konchok Jigmé Namgyal was born in the Markok Valley and was
believed to be the third reincarnation of Ngawang Tenzin Namgyal.
He made a connection with many masters and holy beings, especially
Lhundrub Gyatso, who was also his brother in a previous life. As well
as the Kalachakra teachings, he also mastered the teachings of the
Dakini Neguma and attained many extraordinary qualities as a result
of his flawless study and practice. He mainly taught at Lower Tsangwa
Monastery, in keeping with the tradition of the 'Tsangwa tulku' being
the main holder of the Jonang Kalachakra lineage. Literally Konchok
means 'rare and sublime' and Jigmé means 'fearless'.
Ngawang Chöpel Gyatso, also known as Kunga Chöpel, was born in
1788 and trained at Dzamthang Monastery from the age of ten. He
studied under many teachers and first received the transmission for the
Six Vajra Yogas from Lama Ngawang Gyaltsen at the age of 22. Attaining
great realisation of the first two yogas during a three-year retreat, he
later received the complete transmission from Jigmé Namgyal. He also
received teachings such as Dzogchen and the Six Yogas of Neguma
from many other lamas, and he became known for his extraordinary
clairvoyant abilities. Later in his life Chöpel Gyatso taught and traveled
widely, and he was one of the teachers of the great Rimé masters Jamgon
Kongtrul and Patrul Rinpoche.
He passed away in 1865, and many rainbows appeared in the sky after
his death as testimony to his great realisation. Chöpel means “superior
holder of Dharma'.
Ngawang Chökyi Pakpa was born in 1808 in the Zuka region and was
ordained by Konchok Jigmé Namgyal when he was seven years old. He
was especially proficient in the practice of the first two Vajra Yogas. While
200



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
on retreat he once experienced visions of the Rigden King Pundarika
and Kunkyen Dolpopa, and also perceived visions of Shambala and the
pure-realm of Sukhavati19. At the age of 25 he studied over 100 mandalas
and memorised all of their details. The mandalas and rituals carried out
in Jonang monasteries today can therefore be attributed to him. Chökyi
Pakpa also built a great hall at Dzamthang, and passed away in 1877
without any sign of sickness or pain.
I pray to Ngawang Chöjor Gyatso.
I pray to Ngawang Chözin Gyatso.
· I pray to Ngawang Tenpa Rabgyé.
• Ngawang Chöjor Gyatso was born in 1846 and received the Kalachakra
teachings and empowerment from Tsangwa Gelong. On one occasion
when receiving this empowerment he saw the lama as Kunkyen Dolpopa
and experienced the non-dual Buddha-mind. He diligently practised the
Six Vajra Yogas and attained many great realisations, including magical
powers in his dreams and the continuous perception of his body in the
state of clear-light. At the age of forty-five he became Kalachakra master
at Tsangwa Monastery. He passed away in 1910.
Ngawang Chözin Gyatso was considered to be an emanation of
Akashagarbha, one of the eight great bodhisattvas. He studied at
Dzamthang monastery, where he received all the instructions for the
Six Vajra Yogas, principally from Tsangwa Gelong. He composed many
ritual practices and commentaries, and once revealed that millions of
deities were emanating from his body. His realisation was so profound
that he could perform miraculous feats such as walking through walls
and could travel to pure-realms such as Shambala, receiving instructions
there which he brought back to the Tibetan monasteries. After his death
mantras of the 636 Kalachakra deities appeared inscribed on his bones.
Ngawang Tenpa Rabgyé was born in the year of the Wood Pig. He
received all the instructions for the Six Vajra Yogas from Ngawang
Chözin and experienced many signs indicating mastery of the practice.
He also practised many other tantras and beheld innumerable visions of
the different tantric deities. At 25 he studied and practised at Dzamthang
19 Sukhavati is the pure-realm or ‘Buddha-field' of the Buddha Amitabha,
who vowed to attain enlightenment so that beings may be freed from samsara if they
remembered him at the moment of death.
201



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
way.
monastery, at 56 he became abbot of Chayul monastery, and then later
on he became abbot and Kalachakra master at Chothang Monastery. He
lived a very humble life and had no concern whatsoever for wealth or
social standing. At 76 he passed away, remaining in the state of clear-
light for six days.
I pray to the dispeller of darkness, the precious Lama Lobsang
Trinley.
I pray to the dharma warrior Khentrul Jamphal Lodrö.
I pray to my primary root lama.
Lama Ngawang Lobsang Trinley was born in the Zuka valley of
the Kham region in south-eastern Tibet in 1917. At the age of 14 he
studied at Chayul Monastery under Ngawang Tenpa Rabgyé. He
focused intensively on Kalachakra practice and attained the ten signs of
realisation within two weeks20. In his thirties he contracted leprosy and
therefore entered into solitary retreat for five years, practising Vajrapani.
While in retreat, his sickness appeared in the form of thousands of
worms, flowing out of his body, dissolving and then transforming into
tormas21. Thereafter he spent the rest of his life treating and curing many
people with leprosy and other ailments, and worked tirelessly to rebuild
Mahayana and Vajrayana Buddhism in its pure form and re-establish
Chothang Monastery. He died in 1999, fulfilling his own prediction,
even though he was completely healthy. After thirteen days his body
showed no sign of decay, and many miraculous signs accompanied his
death. He sent all of his precious relics to Potala Palace in Lhasa before
his passing, keeping not a single relic in his own monastery.
Khentrul Jamphal Lodrö was born on 18th day of the second month in the
year of the Water Rabbit. His family lived in a nomadic community in the
Golok province of Eastern Tibet. He was recognised as a reincarnation
of his mother's teacher Getse Khentrul, his previous life's Kalachakra
master Chözin Gyatso,
20 The ten signs of realisation are experienced during the Kalachakra dark
room practice, and correspond to the dissolution of the elements of the body at the
moment of death. They include: smoke, mirage, clouds, fire-flies, sunlight, moonlight,
blazing gemstones, eclipse, starlight and rays of light.
21 Tormas are small offering cakes used in tantric rituals.
202



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND. PATH
-
-
fulfilling his prediction that he would return to his mother. At the age
of twelve he began his extensive Buddhist studies and practice under
the guidance of Khenpo Sangten and several other lamas. He attended
eleven monasteries in Tibet to comprehensively study the five traditions
and undertook a three year Kalachakra retreat under the guidance of his
main master Lama Lobsang Trinley at Chothang monastery, and in 1997
was awarded the title of Rimé Master. In 1999 he was chosen as teacher
at Dzamthang monastery, and then spent time in solitary retreat practice
before undertaking a pilgrimage to India in 2000 to practice at many
sacred Buddhist sites. He arrived in Australia in 2003 after several private
audiences with His Holiness the Dalai Lama and the current Jonang.
leader Khalkha Jetsun Dhampa, with the goal of transmitting the rare
and precious Kalachakra teachings and establishing the Jonang and Rimé
traditions. The title ‘Khentrul means both Dharma scholar and monastic
leader (khen) and recognised reincarnation (trul), while “Jamphal Lodrö’
signifies the gentle and glorious Manjushri, the bodhisattva of wisdom.
Khentrul Jamphal Lodrö with great perserverance is learning the English
language while striving with immense commitement to translate volumes
of Dharma into English.
I pray to my glorious lama.
I pray to all the dharma lords.
May all the spiritual fathers and heart-sons bless me!
Whoever remembers the noble lama will find peace from the suffering
nature of samsara.
Whoever honours and has life-long devotion to the precious lama
will accomplish primordial wisdom and supreme compassion.
The first of these two verses summons us to have an attitude of deep
respect and honour for the lama and all the lineage masters, or dharma
lords. This includes the spiritual fathers and their heart-sons, as the
lineage has been transmitted from spiritual father to son from one
generation to the next. Lama refers not just to one 'root' teacher, but to
anyone from whom you receive initiations or teachings. The final verse
is a reminder of the benefits of remembering these lamas and cultivating
devotion or gratitude towards them. Just remembering them can remind
you of your own good qualities and bring you peace. Having gratitude
and life-long devotion to them, meanwhile, will lead to even greater
benefit. From an ordinary perspective, gratitude and appreciation will
203



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
be a cause of your own happiness. Such gratitude can also grow into
extraordinary devotion and lead you to enlightenment, which is what we
mean by primordial wisdom and supreme compassion (literally 'warrior
compassion').
In all my future lives may I never be separated from
my glorious lama.
May I have great joy in my practice of the precious Dharma.
May I accomplish all enlightened paths and swiftly attain
the state of Vajradhara!
When you pray that you will never be separated from your glorious
lama, you are showing your teachers great honour and devotion. Also,
if you have a strong connection or karmic link with your teachers and
dharma friends, you are likely to meet them again lifetime after lifetime.
· If you are not separated from the Sangha (which includes all great Arya
beings as well as any aspiring practitioner who follows the Buddha), you
will never be separated from the precious Dharma and will have great
joy practising it. You will then gradually accomplish all enlightened
paths, traversing through the various levels of accomplishment22, and
finally attain the state of Vajradhara, or complete enlightenment.
[Be resolute that the lamas of the holy lineage melt into light and bless your mind-
stream)
All of these preliminary practices have two stages – building up a
visualisation and connecting to the theme of the practice, and then
dissolving what you have built up,
recognising that it is all just a construction of your own mind. In this
case the lamas of the holy lineage, which you have focused on in your
visualisation, dissolve or melt into light and then bless your mind-stream,
becoming inseparable from your own mind.
22 . The path to enlightenment is mapped out in great detail and includes many
levels which a practitioner can learn to recognise. For a detailed presentation of these
levels see chapter nine in Unveiling Your Sacred Truth: The Path to Liberation and its
Benefits.
204



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
In the visualisation stage you train your mind at the level of relative
truth, while in the second stage you learn to recognise the ultimate empty
nature of these appearances.

HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
PART TWO: Inner Preliminaries
I. Refuge & Prostrations
Introduction
Refuge is the first of five inner preliminary practices. After contemplating
the four convictions of renunciation, you are left with a sense of 'fear' of
remaining in samsara along with hope that liberation is actually possible
if you place your trust and faith in the Three Jewels. This means having
faith in the Buddha as your guide, the Dharma as the teachings you follow
and the Sangha as your spiritual companions. Without taking refuge it is
not possible to follow the Buddha's path to enlightenment, and therefore
we say that refuge is the foundation of all paths23.
Taking refuge means creating a spiritual link between yourself and the
great holy beings who represent the state of Buddhahood, and committing
to following the teachings which they have transmitted through an
authentic lineage. We can therefore think of the Buddha as a doctor,
the Dharma as the medicine he prescribes for you, and the Sangha as
the nurses who help care for you. This Sangha includes both the Arya
Sangha, those beings who have directly seen the truth of emptiness and
are on a one-way track to enlightenment, and the ordinary Sangha of
spiritual companions or friends. It is up to you alone, however, to take
the medicine as instructed by practising the Dharma.
23 The meaning of this refuge practice is explained in much greater detail in
chapter thirteen of Unveiling Your Sacred Truth: Discovering the Three Jewels by
Taking Refuge. Details include different motivations for taking refuge, different kinds
of faith and the meaning of the Buddha, Dharma and Sangha according to different
levels of Buddhist understanding.
207



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
-
--
.
-
.
-.-
.
.-
We can speak of two types of refuge - relative refuge and ultimate refuge.
On a relative level you make prayers and prostrations to the Buddha,
Dharma and Sangha with strong faith and the motivation to liberate all
beings. Faith means having total trust in the teachings as this allows the
blessings of the refuge to enter within you, while the highest motivation
for taking refuge is to liberate all beings from samsara. On an ultimate
level you are taking refuge in your own Buddha-nature, or your own
three Buddha-kayas24, using the outer refuge as a mirror to see the inner
Buddha.
The practice which follows has two parts – establishing the refuge
visualisation and then reciting the refuge prayer while making full
prostrations.
The details of the refuge visualisation may seem overwhelming at first.
However, many levels of meaning are embedded within these details
and each of these details has profound spiritual significance that we
need to keep intact. It may take a great deal of practice, however, to
unlock this deeper meaning. The visualisation should be live, vibrant,
clear and sealed with the idea of non-duality. If you have difficulty with
the visualisation, you must try your best or at least believe or feel that
the objects of refuge are actually right there in your presence. What is
most important, however, is to be aware of the meaning of the refuge
practice25.
Once you have established the visualisation, you should recite the long
refuge prayer once while holding this in your mind, and then repeat the
short refuge prayer at least three times while making full prostrations. In
the Jonang tradition these prostrations are done slowly, and you change
the position of your hands and your body as you recite each line of the
six-line refuge prayer. While you are performing these prostrations,
24 The three buddha-kayas (sometimes called the ‘inner Three Jewels) are
the dharmakaya truth body, the sambhogakaya enjoyment body and the nirmanakaya
emanation body. The ultimate refuge is seeing that you already possess these three
kayas as part of your Buddha-nature.
25 There are many different levels of refuge, which are in accord with the
views of Theravada, Mahayana and Vajrayana Buddhism. These are described in
chapter 13 of Unveiling Your Sacred Truth: Discovering the Three Jewels by Taking
Refuge.
208



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
you should fill your mind with extraordinary thoughts, remembering all
the amazing qualities of these precious Three Jewels.

The first step is to try
and dissolve all ordinary

feeling of openness', for example, by focusing on the space around you
or resting your mind at the heart-centre at the end of the out-breath.

200
In the centre there is a great palace made of various precious
substances and adorned with excellent ornaments. In the centre of
the palace is a wish-fulfilling tree with its trunk, branches, leaves,
flowers and fruit. At the centre of this tree is a vast, magnificent
throne supported by lions. Upon this throne is a multi-coloured lotus
disc, sun disc and moon disc.
209



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
The precious substances and ornaments which adorn the great palace
symbolise the perfection and purity of the surroundings. The wish-
fulfilling tree represents a firmly rooted foundation and the oneness of
all enlightened beings, with many different aspects (the branches, leaves
and flowers) being displayed to fulfill the wishes of all beings. The lion
throne is a symbol of majesty and power, while the lotus represents purity
and the sun disc and moon disc symbolise wisdom and compassion.
One's root lama is seated upon the throne in the form of Vajradhara
surrounded by all the lineage lamas and the assembly of yidam
deities. At the base of this tree is an ocean of dakinis and all-seeing
dharmà protectors who guard the precious teachings, the eye. They
abide in a manner protecting me.
Vajradhara is the tantric form of enlightenment, as previously described
in the lineage master invocation. He represents the enlightened mind
of your root lama, whether this be one lama or many, and this root
lama is given this most important position as he is your human link
to enlightenment. Earlier we focused specifically on the lineage
lamas; now we also include yidam deities, which are tantric Buddha-
forms mostly wrathful in appearance, who help you accomplish tantric
realisation. Dakinis, known as khandro in Tibetan and literally meaning
‘sky walkers,' are divine feminine forms with the ability to assist genuine
practitioners. They embody a type of spiritual energy which safeguards
your spiritual progress and overcomes inner obstacles to your practice.
Dharma protectors, meanwhile, are wrathful forms that shield you from
external obstacles and harmful forces; they embody a type of spiritual
energy which prevents negativity from coming in, like an iron fence
around you. The dakinis and dharma protectors surround you like an
ocean, ensuring that you always receive spiritual protection.
In each of the four directions surrounding the tree, upon the ground
of the palace, there is a lion throne with a lotus, sun disc and moon
disc. In front of the lama are the supreme emanation bodies of the
Buddhas of the three times and ten directions such as the glorious
victor Buddha Shakyamuni.
On his right side is the Arya Sangha of eight bodhisattvas, including
Maitreya, Manjushri and Avalokiteshvara. To the left is the Arya
210



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
-
.
.
Sangha of shravakas and pratyekas such as Shariputra. Behind him
the holy Dharma appears as precious golden texts.
Extending beyond the dakinis and dharma protectors, who immeditately
surround Vajradhara, are the Buddhas, the Arya Sangha of bodhisattvas
and shravakas, and the Dharma in the form of texts.
The Buddhas are those fully enlightened beings who are all-knowing
and omnipresent. They appear in the three times - the past, present and
future - and the ten directions - the four cardinal directions, the four
intermediate directions, as well as up and down - according to the merit
of beings. This includes the current Buddha Shakyamuni as well as
previous and future Buddhas such as Dipankara and Maitreya.
The Arya Sangha of bodhisattvas are those on the path to buddhahood
who have directly realised the profound view of emptiness, such as
the bodhisattva of compassion Avalokiteshvara and the bodhisattva of
wisdom Manjushri. The sole intention of these sublime beings is to
lead all beings to Buddhahood, so you can regard them as your personal
spiritual guides and protectors. We also take refuge in the Arya Sangha
of Shravakas and Pratekyas. Shravakas, also known as hearers, hear the
Buddha's teachings and attain the state of Arhat or individual liberation,
following the path that is practised today in the Theravada tradition.
Pratyekas, also called solitary realisers, find their own liberation through
analysing the truth of dependent origination without relying directly on
the teachings of a Buddha.
Finally, the Dharma jewel is represented by precious golden-coloured
texts, and you can picture them resonating with the beautiful sound of
the Dharma, especially the definitive teachings on Buddha-nature and
the glorious Kalachakra Tantra.
Be resolute that everything you visualise actually is like this. At the
same time, be resolute that you and all beings take refuge together,
with great longing and devotion towards the lama, the Three Jewels
and the ocean of spiritual protection.
Even if you cannot recall all the details, you should be resolute that
everything you visualise actually is like this and is not just an exercise in
211



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
imagination. As someone who has entered the Mahayana path, you are
not taking refuge just on your own but together with all beings, who are
intimately connected with you as they have been your mothers, partners,
friends and relatives over countless previous life-times. You can therefore
visualise your father on your right side, your mother on your left side,
your adversaries in front of you (given this position of honour as they
have helped you develop patience), and hidden harmful forces at your
rear. Extending this visualisation to every conceivable being in samsara,
you lead them all to take refuge together in the Three Jewels. The ocean
of spiritual protection refers to the entire refuge assembly – the root
lama, lineage lamas, yidams, dakinis, dharma protectors and the Arya
Sangha altogether.
While you are visualising, you must be sure to remember that the lama,
yidams, Buddhas and so forth are not something external or theistic.
Rather, each is a reflection of an important aspect of your own Buddha-
nature, appearing in different forms to guide you although they are one
in their wisdom nature.
Then pray with strong compassion and resolute intention to liberate
all beings, fervently wishing that they find protection from the
suffering of samsara.
As a Mahayana practitioner you are taking refuge not only because you
are seeking freedom from samsara, but because you wish that all beings
find protection from the suffering of samsara. As you perform the refuge
practice you can therefore pray fervently with strong compassion and
resolute intention: ‘I wish they could be free... If only they could be
free... I will help them find freedom... I pray to the Three Jewels that
they find freedom!'
[While holding this visualisation as best you can, recite the long refuge prayer
once and then repeat the short refuge prayer three or more times while making full
prostrations]:
Long Refuge Prayer
[Recited once]
212



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
.
.
For the sake of all mother-like beings as limitless as space,
From now until I reach the essence of enlightenment I take refuge in
the glorious lama,
The dharma lord who embodies the qualities and actions of the
Buddhas of the three times and ten directions,
And is the source of the 84,000 dharmas and king of the noble Arya
Sangha.
There is not a single being in samsara that has not been our mother
since beginningless time, and as our mothers they have loved us with
every possible kindness, tenderness, care and affection. Just as space is
limitless, so too is the number of kind mother-like beings, who can be
found everywhere just like space. It is for their sake that you take refuge
until you reach enlightenment.
In this practice we consider the lama, our human teacher(s) who is our
direct connection to enlightenment, to be the perfect object of refuge
since he embodies the qualities and actions of all the Buddhas and is the
vessel through which we hear the Dharma. He is therefore the link to the
84,000 dharmas that the Buddha taught as a remedy for 84,000 mental
defilements which emerge from the three root afflictions – attachment,
aversion and ignorance. The lama is also king of the Noble Arya Sangha,
since he is our link to countless high-level beings who have the power
to protect and guide us.
(iii) Short Refuge Prayer
[to accumulate with prostration]
I take refuge in the dharma lord, the glorious lama.
I take refuge in the enlightened mandala of yidams.
I take refuge in the bhagavans, the perfect Buddhas.
I take refuge in the immaculate holy Dharma.
I take refuge in the noble Arya Sangha.
I take refuge in the dakinis and all-seeing dharma protectors.
[To be recited three times, or more if you are focusing on refuge practice].
We recite this verse once during each prostration. While reciting I take
refuge in the dharma lord, the glorious lama, the hands are cupped
213



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
together and placed at the forehead. Then while taking refuge in the
yidams the hands are placed at the throat and while taking refuge in
the Buddhas they are placed at the level of the heart. The other lines
are recited through the rest of the prostration, as you touch the floor
first with your hands and then your knees, extending your entire body
along the floor with arms outstretched above your heads, palms flat and
forehead touching the ground. Rising again to a standing position, you
can think of the suffering of all mother-like beings and aspire to work
tirelessly for their benefit. This completes one
prostration26
In the Jonang tradition sessions of the refuge practice last up to two
hours, and the prayer and prostrations are usually done together for a
total of 100,000 times, accumulated over a number of sessions. This
practice affirms our surrender and commitment to the Three Jewels and
is also an effective way to destroy our pride. After a session is completed
the following verse is recited:
I pay homage and take refuge in the lama and
the precious Three Jewels.
Please bless my mind-stream!
[This request is repeated three times).
With this verse you are making a transition to the final part of the practice,
as you ask the lama and the precious Three Jewels to bless your mind-
stream and therefore fill you with all of their good qualities. This mind-
stream, or mind consciousness, will continue to grow in good qualities
until you reach enlightenment. Unlike the five sense consciousnesses,
this mind consciousness can be developed in a limitless way, and this is
what makes enlightenment possible.
[When you finish a session of prostrations and/or refuge prayer recitation, visualise
the objects of refuge melting into light and dissolving into your mind-stream like
water being poured into water].
Be resolute that you become inseparable with the refuge.
26 More details of this practice can again be found in chapter 13 of Unveiling
Your Sacred Truth.
214



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
The final step is to dissolve the field of refuge, visualising all of the
objects melting into light and dissolving into your mind-stream and into
the minds of all other sentient beings as well. This is the ultimate practice
of refuge, through which you learn to recognise that there is no longer
any independent you and them.
This process is usually carried out in seven steps, described in great
detail in Unveiling Your Sacred Truth. First the root and lineage lamas
bless you with dazzling rays of light, and after this you receive blessings
from the yidams, followed by the Buddhas, dharma texts,
Sangha, dakinis and dharma protectors. Finally light radiates from the
entire field of refuge to purify the defilements of all beings, and then
radiates outwards to the Buddha-realms as they all become Buddhas.
The dakinis and dharma protectors then dissolve into the sangha, who
then dissolves into the dharma texts. These texts then dissolve into the
Buddhas, the Buddhas dissolve into the yidams, the yidams dissolve
into the lineage masters and finally these lineage masters dissolve into
the root lama Vajradhara. The vast palace and wish-fulfilling tree also
dissolve into Vajradhara, who then dissolves into your crown chakra and
then abides at your heart chakra.
The idea is to just watch what is happening and try to recognise how
all of these objects are in fact inseparable from your own mind. This
process if likened to water being poured into water, though initially it
may feel much more solid than this. After practising this for a while the
solidity of the visualisation breaks down, and eventually it can feel like
breaking a vase and watching the space inside the vase come together
with the space outside.
(iv) Dedication
Through the power of this virtue,
May I complete the accumulation of merit and wisdom
And so attain the two kayas of enlightenment for
the sake of all beings.
As with any Mahayana practice, you finish by dedicating the virtue or
215



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
merit you have accumulated so that all beings may attain enlightenment.
Merit is the positive energy created by doing this practice or performing
any virtuous deeds with a good motivation. Wisdom, on the other hand,
is to realise that the ultimate nature of all relative phenomena is empty
of true existence, and this is achieved through deep contemplation and
meditation practice. Wisdom and merit are the cause for attaining the
two kayas of enlightenment – the dharmakaya, which is the primordial
wisdom that sees the true nature of all phenomena, and the rupakaya,
which is the compassionate display of enlightened form for the benefit
of all beings. The rupakaya includes both the samboghakaya and
nirmanakaya aspects of the Buddha.
If you fail to dedicate the merit of your practice, this is like leaving
money on a window sill, where it can easily be stolen or blown away
by the wind. Dedicating the merit towards enlightenment, however, is
like investing this money in a bank. It will never be destroyed and will
continue to grow until you reach enlightenment.
216



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
II. Generating the Mind of Awakening
(Bodhicitta)
Introduction
Bodhicitta is the extraordinary altruistic intention to attain enlightenment
for the sake of all beings, and this is the essence of the Mahayana path.
The seed of bodhicitta is compassion, and you must work hard to make
this arise by deeply contemplating certain topics (known as aspiring
bodhicitta) and then putting this into practice (engaged bodhicitta). It is
therefore a precious practice through which you can link your mind with
your heart and also your actions.
To generate aspiring bodhicitta, you must first understand how all
beings are equal to yourself in wanting happiness and wanting to avoid
suffering. This is the basis for developing unconditional love and
compassion towards all beings, regardless of their race, colour or creed,
which embraces all animals and even unseen beings. Furthermore, all
beings have been your dearest ones innumerable times in previous lives
- your mothers, lovers, friends and children. They have therefore been
immensely kind to you countless times, and as you recollect this kindness
it is only natural that you wish to repay it in any way you can.
All your kind mothers, however, are trapped in samsara's great ocean
of suffering, as if caught in a bad dream with no knowledge at all that it
is possible to wake up. Reflecting carefully upon this predicament, you
realise the only way to really help them is to guide or teach them how
to overcome delusion and understand the path to long-lasting happiness.
The altruistic intention of bodhicitta is born when you think in this way -
it is the wish to become enlightened yourself so that you can possess the
all-knowing mind which sees how to best help others in every possible
circumstance, leading them to enlightenment as well. By developing this
intention you are doing something far more valuable than temporarily
healing someone's pain, as you are genuinely planning to help them
217



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
permanently.
All of this is talking about relative or aspiring bodhicitta, yet there is also
ultimate bodhicitta, which is when there is understanding of emptiness.
This is like a fence that surrounds and safeguards your compassion.
You then understand the great paradox that, although you aim to lead
countless beings to enlightenment, there are no (truly existing) beings
to liberate. This means you can abide in a space from which you can
naturally act to benefit others, as you know that any concept of the doer,
the good deed and the problem are all in the mind. Success and failure
to help is also in the mind, so there is no possibility of getting worn out
and no room for views that are goal-oriented or moralistic. This type of
outlook allows you to become a bodhisattva, a warrior of compassion.
Once your attitude has started to transform through your practice
of aspiring bodhicitta, you should then put this into practice in order
to develop engaged bodhicitta. This means taking your practice of
compassion out into the world by volunteering to help homeless or sick
people, or perhaps changing your routine at work so you can apply the
bodhicitta teachings more effectively. In this setting you can practise
what are known as the six perfections – generosity, discipline, patience,
diligence, concentration and wisdom.
Normally we always think about doing something for people whose
suffering is obvious - those with cancer, those with disabilities or those
who are nearing death. However, these teachings also apply in dealing
with those who are rich, arrogant or successful. You can generate
compassion for them too, realising that success only means they are
burning up merit from their karmic bank account, and they are unlikely
to have any opportunity to think about Dharma. You should therefore be
able to develop equal compassion for everone you encounter - those who
are suffering as well as those who are successful. If you are jealous or
think they are “lucky', your bodhicitta still needs work!
In the practice which follows you recite and contemplate the meaning
of two prayers which help you develop the bodhicitta intention, with the
Three Jewels as your witness. Ideally you should supplement this with a
great deal of study and contemplation, remembering that monks usually
spend several years studying the topic of bodhicitta following many
218



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
different texts27. Most important are mind training practices such as
tong-len (-taking and sending'), as presented in chapter 15 of Unveiling
Your Sacred Truth, as well as gaining familiarity with the bodhisattva
vows in chapter 14. Those who are genuinely committed should devote
at least a few months of intensive practice to these contemplations, or
as long as it takes to become familiar with the essential points using
analytical meditation.
The Bodhicitta Practice with Commentary
You can visualise the field of refuge as a support for the bodhicitta
practice, and you can also take the bodhisattva vows in their presence,
pledging to enlighten all beings with the Three Jewels as your witness. It
is beneficial to include the refuge visualisation in your practice, though it
is not essential and depends on your time and ability. Before commencing
this practice you must have already taken refuge at least three times and
tried to mix your mind with the objects of refuge. Having generated
the mind of awakening, now expand it to include all beings without
exception. You can then start the bodhicitta prayer
For the sake of all beings I will reach the state
of complete Buddhahood;
I shall therefore meditate on the profound vajrayoga path.
[To be repeated three or more times).
In this prayer you pledge to reach the state of complete Buddhahood so
that you can benefit all beings in the greatest and vastest possible way.
The two kayas of Buddhahood contain two aspects – the ultimate benefit
for yourself and the ultimate benefit for others. This latter aspect is the
display of countless form emanations for others' benefit.
This bodhicitta wish is stated beautifully in Shantideva’s The Guide to
the Bodhisattva's Way of Life:
27 The bodhicitta teachings are presented in chapters fourteen and fifteen of
Unveiling Your Sacred Truth. In addition to this, there are many celebrated texts that
you may like to study, for example: Shantideva's Guide to the Bodhisattva's Way of
Life, Atisha's Seven Points of Mind Training and Langri Thankpa’s Eight Verses of
Mind Training. Any of the Dalai Lama's writings is also excellent material.
219



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
-
-
DISE-
May I be a guard for those who are protectorless,
A guide for those who journey on the road.
For those who wish to go across the water,
May I be a boat, a raft, a bridge.
In order to reach Buddhahood as swiftly as possible you also pledge
to meditate on the profound vajrayoga path of the Six Kalachakra
Yogas, the supreme meditation method of the Jonang tradition using
special postures and practices in a dark room to reach a state of supreme
concentration and insight. If you carry this out with the unshakeable
pure motivation of bodhicitta, this can lead to Buddhahood in as little as
one lifetime.
As in the refuge practice, it is crucial to realise that it is far more important
to take the meaning of the practice to heart rather than reciting this prayer
over and over again. Therefore you do not 'accumulate the bodhicitta
prayer, but rather use it as a platform from which to contemplate the
bodhicitta teachings. You can do this beforehand, afterwards or at any
time.
[Having generated the mind of awakening, now expand it to include all beings without
exception]:
May all beings have happiness and the causes of happiness.
May all beings be free from suffering and the causes of suffering.
May all beings never be separated from the sublime happiness
that is free from suffering.
May all beings abide in the great equanimity
free from attachment and aversion.
[This prayer is repeated one or three times, or more if you are focusing on bodhicitta
practice).
[After reciting the bodhicitta verses, meditate carefully upon their profound
meaning, and at the end of the session dedicate the virtue of your practice with
whatever dedication prayer you choose].
Wishing all beings happiness and the causes of happiness is known as
immeasurable love, and wishing that they become free from suffering
and the causes of suffering is immeasurable compassion. Wishing that
they never be separated from happiness is immeasurable joy, while
wishing that they abide in great equanimity free from attachment and
220



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
aversion is immeasurable equanimity or impartiality.
Together these are known as the four immeasurables, and they describe
how to arouse bodhicitta so that it expands to embrace all beings without
exception. They are called 'immeasurable' as they are based on an
immeasurably vast motivation, are directed towards an immeasurable
object (all beings), occur over an immeasurable length of time and lead
to an immeasurable result, the attainment of Buddhahood.
When meditating upon any of these qualities, it is advised to begin with
the cultivation of equanimity - otherwise your love and compassion may
be biased or partial. You should begin by bringing to mind someone close
to you, a neutral person and an adversary, reflecting how they equally
want happiness, just like you. Then you begin by thinking of someone
close to you, cultivating a feeling of warmth and a genuine wish that
they find happiness and its causes, including all virtuous thoughts and
actions as well as faith in the path to liberation. You then gradually
expand this circle of love to neutral people, adversaries and finally all
beings, thinking of 'four links' - I wish they could find happiness and
its causes... if only they could find happiness and its causes... I will lead
them to find happiness its causes!... I pray to the Three Jewels that this
be accomplished!
This is how to meditate on immeasurable love, and you follow a similar
process for compassion, joy and equanimity28. At the end of a session,
if you are visualising the field of refuge as witness to your bodhicitta
practice, you should dissolve these objects of refuge and watch them
become inseparable from your own mind. Finally, you should dedicate
the virtue you have accumulated to the enlightenment of all beings, as
with the refuge practice.
28 There are many effective step-by-step methods to contemplate these four
immeasurable states, and a very simplified version is presented here. See chapter
fifteen of Unveiling Your Sacred Truth or the bodhicitta chapter of Guide to Words of
My Perfect Teacher, by Khenpo Nakchung. For beginners, the Dalai Lama has written
a simple book describing such practices called How to Expand Love: Widening the
Circle of Loving Relationships (edited by Jeffrey Hopkins).
221



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE

Vajrasattva practice allows you to uncover the truth of your Buddha-
nature, which is currently

gives you a powerful method

B
What is it that we need to
Vajrasattva Yab-yum purify? Currently we are colonised
by
negative emotions. In addition,
we are controlled by karma or strong habits developed over countless
lifetimes. Often we do not consider the role of negative karma in causing
our misfortunes or obstacles we encounter, as this is hidden from
our normal sphere of awareness - what we normally think causes us
happiness or suffering are temporary conditions and not the root causes.
Furthermore, we have certain karmic propensities that block us from
gaining a good understanding of Dharma or engaging effectively in
certain practices (such as the six vajrayogas). According to Vajrayana
Buddhism, all of these defilements or karmic seeds are stored as bad
energy or 'knots' in your subtle body, and therefore you can ‘wash away'
222



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
these defilements by performing this unique visualisation practice. If all
these karmic seeds are purified they will not ripen in the future - this will
help you progress much more effectively along the spiritual path.
To wash something dirty we need water and soap. When we wash away
negative karma, we need four conditions, known as the four powers:
1. Power of Regret
This is about genuinely acknowledging all your negative tendencies
without concealing anything. You completely expose your pride and
reveal everything in front of Buddha Vajrasattva. You acknowledge
that your past negative actions were very unwise, and honestly
‘lay on the table’ any actions you have committed that resulted in
suffering or were motivated by greed, hatred or carelessness. It is
best to think that your previous negative actions are like a lethal
poison which you have swallowed, and that you must practice the
antidote without delay. You should therefore generate a feeling of
extreme urgency to rid yourself of this lethal poison.
In Western culture people sometimes suffer from excessive guilt or
self-criticism. Remember, however, that you have a completely pure
Buddha-nature underneath all of this which is never harmed by these
stains, full of unconditional love for yourself and all beings.
2. Power of Support
The support for this practice of purification is the healing power and
purity of your Buddha-nature, manifesting in the form of a radiant,
translucent white deity called Vajrasattva. Literally Vajrasattva
means “enlightened warrior' or 'the embodiment of the indestructible
energy of enlightenment’. There are many details in the visualisation
practice that follows, but the essential point is to really be aware of
Vajrasattva's presence and your personal connection with him. You
must therefore understand how Vajrasattva's nature is inseparable
from the nature of your root lama, who is your own personal
connection to enlightenment. If your reliance upon Vajrasattva is
strong and stable, the purification will then be powerful.
223



BY KHENTRUL RINPOCHE
3. Power of Action (as an Antidote)
In this practice the 'antidote’ is when you visualise your body
being cleansed by milk-like healing nectar, washing away all your
impurities, while at the same time you recite the Vajrasattva mantra.
Besides this special practice, there are many other things you can
do to make sure your karma is being purified. You can strive hard to
perform good actions, be kind and compassionate around others and
make amends with those you are in conflict with, doing your best
to be open and patient, and to genuinely ask for forgiveness when
appropriate. Whatever antidote or purification method you choose
to apply, you should be sure to dedicate it towards the purification
process and carry it out with great awareness and urgency.
4. Power of Resolution
This means you should promise not to repeat all those negative
actions you have committed, having confessed them and requested
purification. It is best if you make a determined vow never to repeat
the action again, even if your life is at stake. A strong and sincere
resolve is said to be powerful enough to purify many lifetimes
worth of negative karma. Practically speaking, it is also good if our
resolve is specific - you can promise to avoid a negative behaviour
for a specific period of time or at least promise that you will exert
substantial effort to avoid repeating it.
Prior to commencing the practice that follows, you must first take refuge
in the Three Jewels and generate bodhicitta, as previously described.
With this as your foundation you can begin the actual practice.
224



HIDDEN TREASURE OF THE PROFOUND PATH
